## Base Genre Archetypes

Classic genre reconstructions — the human timeline of game evolution.

De... | sketch | Single verb (sow) with positional consequences — elegant depth from one action. |

Shannon's 1950 paper on chess p... | sketch | Battle Chess added personality to captures — the game is the same, the spectacle is new. |
| 005 | solitaire | card sorting — build four foundation piles by suit (Ace to King) by arranging cards in descending alternating-color tableau columns | ★★★★★ | 1 (Search & Symbols — combinatorial search, game trees) | Klondike has approximately 8×10^67 possible deals. Only about 79% of deals ar... | sketch | Bundled with Windows 3.0 (1990) to teach mouse skills — drag and drop, click, double-click. Became the most-played computer game in history. The game's mix of luck and skill keeps it endlessly replayable. |
| 006 | mahjong | tile matching — remove matching pairs of free tiles from a layered pyramid layout until all tiles are cleared | ★★★★☆ | 2 (Expert Systems — pattern matching, search) | A tile is "free" only if it has no tiles on top and at least one open side. T... | sketch | Brodie Lockard created the computer version in 1981. Shanghai (1986) by Activision made it a mainstream hit. The layered tile layout creates a 2.5D spatial puzzle from 2D matching rules. |
| 008 | go | territory encirclement — place black and white stones on a grid to surround territory and capture opponent groups by removing their liberties | ★★★★★ | 6 (Deep Learning — AlphaGo, Monte Carlo tree search + deep neural networks) | Go has 10^170 legal positions — more than atoms in the observable universe. T... | sketch | The deepest game ever created. Simple rules (place stones, surround to capture) create extraordinary strategic depth. The ko rule prevents infinite loops. Territory counting determines the winner. |
| 009 | reversi | flanking capture — place discs to outflank opponent's pieces in any direction; flanked pieces flip to your color | ★★★★☆ | 4 (Expert Boom — Logistello defeated world champion 1997; positional evaluation) | Corner squares are maximally valuable (can never be flipped), edges are stron... | sketch | "A minute to learn, a lifetime to master." Four starting pieces in center. Must outflank at least one opponent piece to play. If no legal move, turn passes. Most pieces when board is full wins. |
| 010 | backgammon | dice race with blocking — roll dice to move checkers around the board; block opponent with stacks; bear off first to win | ★★★★★ | 4-5 (Neural Revival → Statistical Learning — TD-Gammon by Gerald Tesauro, temporal difference learning) | TD-Gammon (1992) achieved expert-level play through self-play and temporal di... | sketch | One of the oldest continuously played games. The doubling cube (1920s) added a gambling/negotiation layer. Movement is deterministic given dice, but dice create probability. |
| 011 | checkers | jump capture — move diagonally on dark squares; jump over opponent pieces to capture them; reach the back row to become a king | ★★★★★ | 1-5 (Search & Symbols → Statistical — Samuel 1959 first self-learning AI; Chinook solved checkers 2007) | Arthur Samuel's 1959 checkers program was the FIRST game AI that learned from... | sketch | 12 pieces per side on 8×8 board (only dark squares used). Regular pieces move diagonally forward; kings move both directions. Must capture if possible (forced capture rule). Multi-jump chains allowed. |
| 012 | dominoes | number chain matching — play tiles by matching their pip values to the open ends of the chain; first to empty your hand wins | ★★★★☆ | 2 (Expert Systems — rule-based play, probabilistic tracking of unseen tiles) | Dominoes with hidden tiles is a game of imperfect information. Expert play re... | sketch | Standard double-six set (28 tiles, values 0-6). Draw game variant: if you can't play, draw from the boneyard until you can. When no one can play and boneyard is empty, lowest pip total wins. |
| 013 | blackjack | risk management — draw cards to reach 21 without going over; beat the dealer's hand; manage bet sizing | ★★★★★ | 2-5 (Expert Systems → Statistical — probability theory, card counting, optimal strategy) | Edward Thorp's 1962 book proved that blackjack could be beaten by tracking de... | sketch | Blackjack is the most widely played casino card game. The rules are simple but optimal play requires memorizing a strategy table. The tension between "hit" (risk busting) and "stand" (risk losing to dealer) is the core decision loop. |
| 014 | fanorona | dual-direction capture — move a piece toward enemies to capture by approach OR move away to capture by withdrawal; chain captures possible | ★★★★☆ | 2 (traditional Malagasy ~1680) | Fanorona's dual-direction capture mechanic creates a unique decision tree whe... | sketch | Madagascar's national board game. The 9x5 board with mixed orthogonal and diagonal connections |
| 015 | surakarta | orbital capture — 6×6 grid with curved corner loops; normal moves are one step; captures ONLY happen by traveling along curved loops to reach enemy pieces | ★★★★☆ | 2 (traditional Javanese court game with unique loop-based capture) | Surakarta's orbital capture mechanic is unique in board game history — pieces... | sketch | The curved loops connecting board edges are the visual and mechanical signature. |
| 016 | patolli | X-shaped race with bean dice — roll marked beans as dice; race pieces around an X-shaped 52-square board; landing on opponents sends them back; first to get all pieces home wins | ★★★★★ | 0 (traditional Mesoamerican — ~200 BCE, no AI lineage) | Patolli predates European contact and represents an independent invention of ... | sketch | Played by Aztecs, Maya, and other Mesoamerican peoples. Nobles wagered gold, jade, and even their freedom. The X-shaped board is unique among ancient race games. |
| 017 | yutnori | stick-throwing race — throw 4 half-round sticks; flat/round combinations determine move distance (1-5 + bonus); race pieces around a square board with shortcut diagonal paths | ★★★★☆ | 1 (Symbolic AI — tree search with stochastic elements) | Yut Nori combines stochastic outcomes (stick throws) with strategic choice (w... | sketch | Traditional Korean New Year game still widely played today. |

| 020 | janggi | Korean chess with cannons — 9×10 board with river and palaces; cannons MUST jump over exactly one piece to move or capture; generals confined to 3×3 palace | ★★★★☆ | 2 (traditional Korean board game, ~900 CE) | Janggi's cannon mechanic — requiring a jump over exactly one piece to move or... | sketch | The cannon jumping mechanic is the unique selling point, creating totally different tactics from any other chess variant. |
| 021 | togyzkumalak | mancala with tuzdyk — 2×9 pits with 162 stones; the tuzdyk rule permanently claims an opponent's pit when capturing exactly 3; alters the board topology permanently | ★★★★☆ | 2 (Expert Systems — heuristic rules, knowledge encoding) | Togyzkumalak introduces a permanent board mutation — the tuzdyk — that has no... | sketch | The tuzdyk mechanic permanently alters board topology — unique among all mancala variants. |
| 022 | congkak | mancala with store-in-circuit — the player's store IS included in the sowing circle; creates capture-your-own-store dynamics; skip opponent's store during sowing | ★★★★☆ | 2 (Knowledge Engineering — rule-based systems, expert heuristics) | Congkak's store-in-circuit sowing creates different tempo dynamics than Afric... | sketch | 2x7 board with 7 seeds per pit. ## AI Archaeology

"What if AI had participated?" — variants that reimagine classic games through the AI lens.

### ADV -- Adventure

| # | Name | Mechanic | Rating | AI Era | AI Insight | Status | Notes |

Each unexplored room c... | sketch | Miyamoto designed Zelda's overworld to reward curiosity — the same curiosity bonus that modern RL agents use (Pathak 2017, Random Network Distillation 2019). The overlay shows the player's implicit curiosity function made explicit. |
| 018 | globe-deduction | detective deduction with constraint analysis — play a Carmen Sandiego-style game while seeing which suspects and destinations are eliminated by each clue | ★★★★☆ | 5 (Statistical Learning — constraint satisfaction, Bayesian elimination) | Carmen Sandiego is a constraint satisfaction problem. Each clue eliminates su... | sketch | The original game taught geography through detective work. This overlay teaches how constraint propagation and probabilistic inference work — the same machinery behind modern AI reasoning systems. |

### ANC -- Ancient Games

| 023 | go-territory | territory encirclement with AI evaluation — play Go while seeing an AI heatmap of territorial value for each intersection | ★★★★★ | 6 (Deep Learning — AlphaGo, MCTS + neural evaluation) | Go's territorial evaluation was the last frontier for AI. The heatmap colors each empty intersection by estimated ownership: green = likely Black territory, red = likely White territory, with intensity reflecting confidence. Shows estimated score difference. Based on influence function radiating from stones with distance decay. |

This prototype bridges 5000 years of play with 65 years of AI: the tree search is classical, the visualization is the archaeology. |
| 026 | blackjack-count | risk management with card counting — play Blackjack while seeing the running count, true count, and mathematically optimal decision for each hand | ★★★★★ | 2-5 (Statistical Methods → Expert Systems — probability calculation, optimal strategy tables, risk assessment) | Edward Thorp proved in 1962 that Blackjack could be beaten with card counting... | sketch | Six-deck shoe, standard Vegas rules (dealer stands on soft 17, double after split, no surrender). | 027 | backgammon-ev | dice race with expected value — play Backgammon while seeing the AI's evaluation of each possible move; accounts for dice probability and opponent responses | ★★★★★ | 4-5 (Neural Networks → Deep Learning — temporal difference learning, position evaluation) | TD-Gammon (1992) learned to play expert-level Backgammon entirely through sel... | sketch | Backgammon's dice element makes it fundamentally different from chess for AI — the branching factor explodes with 21 possible dice rolls per turn. TD-Gammon's breakthrough was evaluating positions rather than searching deep trees. This prototype surfaces that evaluation: the expected value of each legal move, blot exposure risk, blocking potential, and race advantage. |

How AI would evolve games independently — the machine timeline.

## AI Archaeology Summary

### Prototypes by AI Era

**Era 0** — 0 (traditional Mesoamerican — ~200 BCE, no AI lineage)
- `ANC-016 patolli` — Patolli predates European contact and represents an independent invention of race-game mechanics in Mesoamerica. The bean-dice system is a binary random generator — each bean is a Bernoulli trial — making it mathematically elegant despite its simplicity.

**Era 1** — 1 (Symbolic AI — tree search, graph algorithms)
- `ANC-001 ur` — The Royal Game of Ur is a stochastic game with perfect information — the same class as backgammon. Expectiminimax (minimax with chance nodes) is the optimal algorithm. The shared middle track creates blocking and capture — emergent conflict from spatial constraints. TD-Gammon (1992) solved backgammon's cousin using temporal-difference learning, proving that ancient game designers had unknowingly created ideal RL training environments.
- `ANC-002 mancala` — Mancala has a single verb (sow) with positional consequences that cascade. Despite simple rules, the game tree is enormous — a solved game only for small configurations. The sowing mechanic is a discrete dynamical system where each action redistributes state across the board. Multi-step sowing (ending in your store = extra turn) creates lookahead chains that reward computational planning.
- `ANC-004 chess` — Chess is THE canonical problem in AI history. Shannon's 1950 paper on chess programming, Turing's paper machine, and Deep Blue's 1997 victory are milestones that defined each AI era. Every game tree search algorithm was first tested on chess.
- `ANC-005 solitaire` — Klondike has approximately 8×10^67 possible deals. Only about 79% of deals are winnable, but determining winnability is intractable. Players develop heuristics (expose hidden cards, keep foundations even, preserve empty columns) that mirror the pruning strategies AI search algorithms use.
- `ANC-017 yutnori` — Yut Nori combines stochastic outcomes (stick throws) with strategic choice (which piece to move, whether to stack). The bonus throw mechanic for Yut(4) and Mo(5) creates chains of decisions reminiscent of multi-step planning in AI. Stacking pieces is an early example of risk-reward aggregation — concentrating value for speed at the cost of catastrophic capture.
- `ANC-018 hnefatafl` — Hnefatafl's asymmetry makes it a fascinating AI challenge — the two sides have fundamentally different objectives, requiring distinct evaluation functions. Unlike chess where both sides optimize the same goal, here the attacker maximizes encirclement while the defender maximizes escape routes.
- `WLD-001 search-game` — The game IS the search. A* expanding from the goal shows the same exploration the player performs mentally. Era 1 AI was entirely about this — searching through explicit tree or graph structures. Making it visible doesn't make the game easier; it makes the AI's reasoning legible. You understand the algorithm by navigating through it.

**Era 1-5** — 1-5 (Search & Symbols → Statistical — Samuel 1959 first self-learning AI; Chinook solved checkers 2007)
- `ANC-011 checkers` — Arthur Samuel's 1959 checkers program was the FIRST game AI that learned from experience. It used temporal difference learning to improve its evaluation function — the same principle behind TD-Gammon 33 years later. Jonathan Schaeffer's Chinook (2007) solved checkers entirely: perfect play from any position is known.
- `PUZ-018 slidepuzzle` — The 15-puzzle inspired fundamental search algorithms. The Manhattan distance heuristic (sum of each tile's distance from its goal) was one of the first admissible heuristics for A* search. Exactly half of all starting positions are unsolvable — determined by the parity of the permutation.
- `ANC-025 checkers-tree` — Arthur Samuel's 1959 checkers program was the first program to learn from experience. It used alpha-beta pruning with a learned evaluation function — the same tree search visible here. Samuel coined the term "machine learning." The overlay shows the AI's move tree exactly as Samuel's program would have built it.

**Era 1-6** — 1-6 (Search & Symbols → Deep Learning — minimax, alpha-beta, neural evaluation)
- `ANC-007 deepthink` — Chess was AI's original benchmark. Shannon (1950) proposed the minimax algorithm for chess before any computer could play it. The search tree visualization shows the same alpha-beta pruning that Deep Blue used to beat Kasparov — but made legible. Each node is an evaluated position; pruned branches are greyed out. The player sees how the AI thinks.

**Era 1-7** — 1-7 (Search & Symbols → Generative-Agentic — human-computer interaction, motor learning, input bandwidth)
- `SIM-013 typeracer` — Touch typing is the most practiced motor skill in computing. WPM measures the bandwidth of human text output — typically 40-80 WPM for proficient typists, 150+ for champions. This bandwidth constraint fundamentally shapes how humans interact with AI systems. The game measures the bottleneck in human-computer communication.

**Era 2** — 2 (Expert Systems — rule-based parsing and world state)
- `ADV-001 text-adventure` — Text adventures are the original natural language interfaces — Zork's parser was a rule-based expert system that mapped English verbs to world-state mutations, predating modern NLU by decades. The game world is a knowledge graph traversed by symbolic reasoning.
- `ADV-017 folk-thief` — Iljimae represents Korea's earliest original game character, predating most Asian game mascots. The Robin Hood archetype — steal from the rich, give to the poor — creates a moral feedback loop where the player's reward comes not from accumulation but from redistribution. This is an early example of prosocial game mechanics.
- `ANC-003 rank-battle` — Stratego is an imperfect information game where each combat reveals information — players build a probabilistic model of opponent piece identities through observation. This is Bayesian inference applied to adversarial game theory, where each engagement updates the belief state.
- `ANC-006 mahjong` — A tile is "free" only if it has no tiles on top and at least one open side. This creates a visibility-action gap — you can see tiles you can't act on. Planning requires reasoning about which removals unlock future moves. This is a constrained search problem where greedy matching often leads to dead ends.
- `ANC-012 dominoes` — Dominoes with hidden tiles is a game of imperfect information. Expert play requires tracking which tiles remain unseen and inferring opponent's hand from their draws and passes. This is the same hidden-state inference that Bayesian filters perform.
- `ANC-014 fanorona` — Fanorona's dual-direction capture mechanic creates a unique decision tree where every move has two possible interpretations. The approach/withdrawal choice doubles the branching factor compared to simple capture games, making it computationally interesting despite the small board.
- `ANC-015 surakarta` — Surakarta's orbital capture mechanic is unique in board game history — pieces can only be captured by traveling along curved loop paths at the board edges. This creates an entirely different spatial reasoning challenge from any other abstract strategy game.
- `ANC-019 makruk` — Makruk is the closest living relative of the original Indian Chaturanga. Unlike Western chess, it preserved the weak queen and bishop, making it a slower, more positional game where endgame technique is paramount. The lack of long-range diagonal pieces forces close-quarters tactical play.
- `ANC-020 janggi` — Janggi's cannon mechanic — requiring a jump over exactly one piece to move or capture — creates a unique tactical dimension absent from Western chess. The cannon's power fluctuates wildly as pieces are captured, making material evaluation non-trivial for both humans and AI.
- `ANC-021 togyzkumalak` — Togyzkumalak introduces a permanent board mutation — the tuzdyk — that has no parallel in Western mancala variants. Claiming an opponent's pit as your own permanently redirects future stone flow, creating an irreversible topological change. This mechanic encodes a deep heuristic: sometimes the most valuable move is one that alters the rules themselves. Traditional Central Asian nomadic peoples (UNESCO Intangible Heritage 2020).
- `ANC-022 congkak` — Congkak's store-in-circuit sowing creates different tempo dynamics than African mancala. The extra pit and higher seed count expand the game tree considerably. Expert heuristics capture the importance of store-landing bonus turns and opposite-pit captures more naturally than brute-force search.
- `DEF-001 paddle-volley` — Opponent is a Y-tracker if-statement — indistinguishable from contemporary AI capability. The ball's physics creates more emergent intelligence than the opponent's code. Specialization beats generality at every era.
- `DEF-002 brick-break` — No AI opponent at all — physics is the antagonist. DeepMind's DQN (2013) found the tunnel-behind-the-wall strategy humans knew intuitively but never formally documented until AI rediscovered it.
- `FIX-007 gorillas` — Artillery games reduce to parametric projectile physics — angle θ and velocity v₀ define a parabolic arc modified by gravity and wind. The AI opponent solves this inverse kinematics problem with deliberate error injection to simulate human imperfection.
- `MAZ-006 nightstalker` — Night Stalker's enemies have distinct behavioral states (patrol, chase, retreat) creating emergent difficulty through interaction — an early example of multi-agent systems where simple individual rules produce complex collective behavior.
- `MAZ-007 locknchase` — Lock 'n' Chase adds player-created obstacles to the pursuit game — the ability to reshape the maze in real-time transforms a pure evasion problem into a planning problem, presaging dynamic environment manipulation in modern AI research.
- `MAZ-009 snake` — The self-avoiding walk problem is NP-complete. Snake's core challenge — finding a Hamiltonian path that visits every cell — maps directly to one of theoretical CS's hardest problems. Players solve it through spatial intuition that no algorithm of the era could match.
- `PHY-003 lunar-lander` — Lunar Lander is a continuous control problem with a hard constraint (fuel) and a soft constraint (landing speed). It's the archetype of the powered descent guidance problem that NASA solved analytically for Apollo — optimal control theory applied to games before anyone called it that.
- `PLT-008 jungle-swing` — Pitfall's 256 screens were generated by a polynomial counter (LFSR), making it one of the earliest examples of procedural content generation — a technique that would later become central to AI-driven game design.
- `PUZ-020 concentration` — Concentration directly tests spatial working memory — the ability to bind objects to locations and recall them after interference. The game's difficulty scales with grid size in the same way that memory span experiments do. Human chunking strategies (grouping nearby cards) mirror the memory management strategies that cache architectures use.
- `PUZ-037 egg-roll` — Doki Doki Penguin Land inverted the platformer formula by making downward progress the goal, turning gravity from ally into threat. The egg's fragility creates a constraint-satisfaction puzzle where every block-break decision must account for cascading consequences — a design that presages modern physics puzzle games.
- `RAC-001 racer` — Pseudo-3D racing is one of gaming's great optical illusions. No 3D engine, no polygons — just scaled horizontal lines creating the sensation of depth. The road is a mathematical curve rendered as stacked trapezoids. Every racing game before polygon era lived inside this trick.
- `RAC-003 leap-racer` — Bump 'n' Jump's dual mechanic of bumping cars off-road AND jumping over water creates a risk-reward decision at speed — a real-time resource allocation problem where the player must choose between offensive and defensive actions under time pressure.
- `SCR-007 vanguard-scroll` — Vanguard's 4-way fire combined with changing scroll directions forces the player to maintain situational awareness on all axes simultaneously — a multi-directional threat assessment problem that foreshadows sensor fusion in autonomous systems.
- `SCR-013 tube-shooter` — Tempest's lane-based movement is a clever discrete spatial representation — the continuous tube is quantized into segments, reducing the movement problem to a 1D array. This discretization makes enemy AI trivial (climb your lane) while creating emergent complexity through lane-switching Flippers.
- `SHT-002 room-escape` — Evil Otto is an unkillable pursuer with simple deterministic behavior — it bounces and chases. This creates asymmetric threat levels: robots are killable puzzles, Otto is a hard timer. The combination of solvable and unsolvable threats in the same space creates a planning problem where the player must triage — a concept formalized in multi-objective optimization decades later.
- `SHT-003 biplanes` — The biplane dogfight adds gravity to the rotation-thrust model of Asteroids, creating a richer state space where altitude is a depletable resource. The AI opponent uses simple heuristics (turn toward player, maintain altitude) — demonstrating that rule-based agents can create engaging adversaries when physics provides the complexity.
- `SIM-001 trade-voyage` — Taipan is a sequential decision problem under uncertainty — the player optimizes a buy-sell strategy across a stochastic price graph with risk events. This is the multi-armed bandit problem applied to spatial economics, where each port is an arm with non-stationary rewards and the travel cost is the exploration penalty.
- `SPT-019 gonggi` — Gonggi's core challenge is a real-time dexterity test with sequential constraints. The timing window between toss and catch creates a natural difficulty curve as more stones must be gathered in each round. An AI needs only simple timing heuristics — the human challenge is entirely in motor coordination.
- `SPT-021 tuho` — Tuho is a deceptively simple ballistic problem. The narrow pot opening creates a tight constraint on the angle-velocity space. An expert system can solve the optimal throw analytically, but human players must develop muscle memory through repetition — the same motor learning challenge that makes darts and archery compelling.
- `SPT-022 jegichagi` — Jegichagi is a timing-purity game — the entire challenge reduces to a single well-timed input repeated with increasing precision. The rhythm of kick-wait-kick creates a meditative flow state similar to juggling or yo-yo play, where the player internalizes gravity as muscle memory.
- `SPT-023 paengi` — A top-spinning survival game reduces to a timing optimization problem. The ideal whip moment depends on current RPM and friction curve — a heuristic bot can learn the optimal strike window. Wind adds a spatial dimension requiring positional adjustment.
- `SCR-018 scroll-threat` — Vanguard's four-directional shooting creates a unique tactical space where threats surround the player from all sides. The 360° threat radar makes directional danger visible, revealing which firing direction is most urgent. This transforms reactive play into strategic prioritization — the same problem faced by military situational awareness systems and autonomous threat detection.

**Era 2-3** — 2-3 (Expert Systems / Knowledge Explosion — rule-based scheduling, 1965–1983)
- `SRV-002 smartbar` — What if an expert system had designed Tapper's UI? M/M/N queueing theory existed in 1983 — the mathematical tools to model this exact multi-server problem. An expert system would have surfaced urgency scores, lane rankings, and service windows. This version shows what OR-informed game design looked like 20 years before Diner Dash invented it commercially.

**Era 2-5** — 2-5 (Expert Systems → Statistical — probability theory, card counting, optimal strategy)
- `ANC-013 blackjack` — Edward Thorp's 1962 book proved that blackjack could be beaten by tracking dealt cards — information theory applied to gambling. Basic strategy (the optimal play for every hand vs dealer upcard) reduces the house edge to 0.5%. Card counting shifts the edge to the player. The game teaches expected value and risk management through real stakes.
- `DEF-005 whackamole` — Whac-A-Mole directly measures reaction time + motor precision — the two components of Fitts's Law (1954). As moles appear faster, the game transitions from a reaction task to an attention allocation task: you can't watch all holes simultaneously. This is the cocktail party problem — selective attention under information overload.
- `ANC-026 blackjack-count` — Edward Thorp proved in 1962 that Blackjack could be beaten with card counting. The Hi-Lo system assigns +1 to low cards (2-6), 0 to neutral (7-9), and -1 to high cards (10-A). The true count (running count / decks remaining) predicts the house edge. This prototype makes the mathematics visible — every card's impact on the count, the optimal play for every situation, and the expected value of each decision.
- `DEF-004 trajectron` — Pong's original AI was a perfect Y-tracker — the simplest expert system. Breakout's complexity comes from multi-bounce prediction through a field of bricks. The trajectory overlay shows what a physics engine computes trivially but humans find cognitively demanding: where will the ball be after 3-5 bounces? DQN (2013) learned to play Breakout from raw pixels, discovering the tunnel-to-top strategy without being told.

**Era 3** — 3 (Knowledge Explosion — adventure game logic, state machines)
- `ADV-002 crown-adventure` — King's Quest transformed text adventure state machines into spatial puzzles — the dependency graph of items and locks became a physical world the player walks through. This is constraint satisfaction made tangible, where the search space is literal geography.
- `ADV-003 quest-explorer` — Zelda's open world is a directed graph with soft gates — the player can theoretically go anywhere, but difficulty gradients and item requirements create an implicit curriculum. This is reward shaping through world design, decades before the concept was formalized in reinforcement learning.
- `ADV-004 animated-quest` — Dragon's Lair reduced gameplay to a binary tree of success/failure at predetermined branch points — effectively a decision tree, the fundamental data structure of 1980s expert systems. The game IS a playable decision tree, making its connection to contemporary AI unmistakable.
- `ADV-005 haunted-puzzle` — Maniac Mansion's SCUMM engine abstracted adventure game logic into a verb-noun state machine, creating one of the first domain-specific languages for interactive narrative. The verb-object-target grammar is structurally identical to the action-entity-parameter patterns in modern task-planning AI systems.
- `ADV-006 globe-detective` — Carmen Sandiego combined a geographic knowledge database with deductive reasoning gameplay — the player essentially performs a search algorithm through a graph of cities, using clue constraints to prune the suspect space. This is constraint satisfaction in game form.
- `DEF-003 air-puck` — Shufflepuck Cafe gave each opponent a distinct behavioral personality — varying reaction speed, aggressiveness, and strategy. This was an early form of AI personality modeling, where the same basic algorithm is parameterized differently to create perceivably different characters.
- `DEF-003 air-puck` — Shufflepuck Cafe gave each opponent a distinct behavioral personality — varying reaction speed, aggressiveness, and strategy. This was an early form of AI personality modeling, where the same basic algorithm is parameterized differently to create perceivably different characters.
- `FGT-001 flap-duel` — Height-wins is elegant because it reduces combat to a single axis of advantage. The flap mechanic creates emergent difficulty curves — mastering momentum IS mastering the game. No health bars needed when physics determines victory.
- `FGT-002 kung-fu` — Kung-Fu Master's wave spawning system uses simple frequency and direction rules to create escalating difficulty — an early form of procedural difficulty adjustment that anticipated modern dynamic difficulty systems.
- `FGT-003 monster-smash` — Rampage's building destruction uses a tile-based damage model where each section can be independently destroyed, creating emergent collapse patterns. This granular destruction prefigured voxel-based damage systems and physics-driven demolition in modern games.
- `FGT-004 martial-run` — Karateka was the first game to use rotoscoped animation for fluid character movement, filmed from Jordan Mechner's karate-trained father. This human-motion-to-game-animation pipeline prefigured motion capture technology and the pose estimation systems that modern ML uses for animation.
- `FGT-005 arena-fighter` — Fighting game AI must balance between being beatable and being challenging — early fighting game AI like MK's read player inputs with a delay window, creating the illusion of reaction. At higher difficulties, the AI reads inputs faster, effectively cheating — a controversial but effective difficulty scaling technique.
- `FIX-001 invaders` — Speedup-as-aliens-die is emergent curriculum learning — automatic difficulty escalation as the player succeeds. AI researchers wouldn't formalize curriculum learning until ~2015. Taito's accidental bug was 37 years ahead of the field.
- `FIX-003 bug-shoot` — Player actions reshape the battlefield — every shot that hits a centipede segment spawns a mushroom, making future paths less predictable. This is implicit environment co-design between player and game system, decades before the concept of environment shaping in RL.
- `FIX-004 sky-defense` — Missile Command is a resource allocation problem under escalating load — defend N cities with M missiles against increasing ICBM waves. The player must decide which threats to intercept and which to sacrifice. This is the defender's dilemma formalized: limited defense resources against unlimited offense. Game theory's asymmetric warfare before the term existed in gaming.
- `HYB-001 archon` — Archon requires two fundamentally different AI competencies in one game — strategic board evaluation (like chess engines) and real-time combat reflexes (like action game AI). This dual-reasoning architecture prefigures modern hierarchical AI systems that combine strategic planners with reactive controllers.
- `HYB-007 paradroid` — Paradroid's hacking minigame is a real-time resource allocation problem — both sides compete to claim nodes in a circuit graph. The AI must balance offensive activation with defensive blocking, a microcosm of adversarial planning that prefigures modern competitive AI agents.
- `MAZ-001 pellet-chase` — Four ghost personalities are hand-crafted multi-agent behavioral policies, each with a different reward function — cooperative MARL built by intuition 20 years before the formal theory. Reward engineering before the term existed.
- `MAZ-002 road-crossing` — Frogger is a rhythm-matching problem disguised as a maze game — each lane is a timing window. Modern RL agents struggle with Frogger because the reward signal is sparse and the state space demands long-horizon planning across multiple independent timers.
- `MAZ-003 ice-slide` — Pengo's sliding-block crush mechanic is a planning problem where each push has cascading spatial consequences — the player must reason about trajectories through a dynamic obstacle field, anticipating both block physics and enemy pathfinding simultaneously.
- `MAZ-004 tunnel-digger` — Dig Dug's enemies exhibit two behavioral modes — normal pathfinding and ghost-mode wall penetration — an early example of state-machine AI with environment-dependent transitions, predating modern context-aware agent architectures.
- `MAZ-005 light-trail` — Light cycle games create a pure adversarial territory-denial problem. The shrinking free space is a constraint satisfaction problem that becomes harder each frame — prefiguring the bounded-resource reasoning central to real-time AI planning.
- `MAZ-012 tile-puzzle` — Each tile type follows a single deterministic rule, yet their combinations produce puzzles requiring planning, sequencing, and spatial reasoning. This compositional complexity from simple rules mirrors expert system design — small rule bases generating rich behavior.
- `MAZ-013 laberinto` — The knowledge-as-key mechanic transforms trivia into spatial navigation. Each question becomes a lock — correct answers literally open paths forward. This maps knowledge representation onto physical space, a principle used in early expert systems where decision trees guided users through branching problem spaces.
- `MAZ-016 burrow-puzzle` — Mole Mania's two-layer puzzle design anticipates multi-state search problems where the solution requires transitioning between parallel state spaces — a concept now fundamental to hierarchical planning in AI.
- `PHY-001 space-rocks` — Continuous state-space RL problem before RL was applied to games. Shoot-creates-more-threats mirrors the exploration-exploitation tradeoff: shooting is exploitation (points now), not shooting is exploration (safer navigation later).
- `PHY-004 marble-roll` — Marble Madness was the first arcade game to use a full physics simulation for its core mechanic. The trackball-to-momentum mapping presaged modern physics engines and the continuous-state control problems that deep reinforcement learning would later tackle.
- `PHY-005 pinball` — Pinball simulation requires accurate real-time physics — ball-flipper collision response, gravity, friction, and elastic bouncing. Digital Illusions' engine was one of the first to make convincing pinball physics accessible on home computers, demonstrating that realistic simulation could be entertaining.
- `PLT-001 runner` — Variable-height jump is continuous action control — infinite action space per jump. Coin placement is reward shaping: teaching through environmental design, the exact principle behind curriculum learning and reward shaping in RL.
- `PLT-002 dig-and-run` — The absence of jump IS the design — the player must use terrain modification (digging) as both traversal and trap mechanic. This is a planning problem where the action space includes environment modification. Enemies that can be temporarily trapped but regenerate create a temporal constraint on exploration. The level design becomes a puzzle where the solution path must account for dig-fill cycles.
- `PLT-003 whip-climb` — Castlevania's deliberate movement constraints (stiff jumps, commitment to attacks) force the player to plan actions before executing them — each input is a commitment, not a correction. This mirrors the explore-exploit tradeoff where gathering information (observing patterns) must precede action (attacking).
- `PLT-004 bounty-explorer` — Metroid's ability-gated world is a dependency graph disguised as geography — the player solves a topological sort problem by discovering which abilities unlock which paths. This is constraint satisfaction where the constraints are spatial and the variables are capabilities.
- `PLT-005 barrel-climb` — Donkey Kong introduced narrative goal structure to arcade games — the player has a visible objective (rescue) beyond score maximization. This goal-directed behavior model parallels the shift from reactive to planning-based AI agents.
- `PLT-006 bubble-trap` — Bubble Bobble's item system uses hidden counters and specific action sequences to trigger rare power-ups — an early procedural reward system where player behavior unlocks content, prefiguring achievement systems and behavioral analytics in modern game design.
- `PLT-007 palace-runner` — Prince of Persia's rotoscoped animation created the first physics-plausible movement system in platformers — momentum, stumbling, ledge-grabbing. This commitment to physical realism in character control prefigured the motion-capture and physics-based animation that modern AI animation systems now generate.
- `PLT-009 sky-archer` — Kid Icarus combined vertical platforming with RPG elements (health upgrades, weapon strength, shops) — an early example of genre hybridization that would later become standard. The vertical scroll direction was unusual and created unique spatial reasoning challenges.
- `PLT-016 reform-platform` — Perestroika's shrinking platforms create an emergent difficulty curve where the game state itself degrades over time — a mechanic that mirrors entropy in complex adaptive systems and the concept of non-stationary environments in reinforcement learning.
- `PLT-018 elastic-swing` — Umihara Kawase's rubber-band grappling physics emerged from careful spring-damper simulation — the same differential equations that underpin modern physics engines and soft-body AI research.
- `PLT-019 water-blast` — Liquid Kids pioneered real-time fluid simulation as a core gameplay mechanic in 1990. The water bombs create emergent cascading behaviors — water flows downhill, pools in gaps, and sweeps enemies contextually. This represents an early attempt at physics-driven combat where the environment itself becomes the weapon, prefiguring modern physics sandbox games.
- `PLT-020 bounce-throw` — Gimmick!'s bouncing star mechanic is a real-time physics simulation that required players to internalize reflection angles — essentially becoming human trajectory predictors. The emergent puzzle-platforming created by physics interactions anticipated how ML systems learn to predict physical outcomes from observed trajectories.
- `PUZ-004 pyramid-hop` — Q*bert's isometric grid creates a spatial coverage problem — visiting all tiles while avoiding enemies — structurally identical to the graph traversal problems that dominated 1980s AI planning research.
- `PUZ-007 word-grid` — Scrabble AI is a constraint satisfaction problem disguised as a word game. The AI must simultaneously satisfy lexical validity, board connectivity, and tile availability — a combinatorial search problem that early expert systems could solve but humans approach through vocabulary intuition.
- `PUZ-009 box-push` — Sokoban is PSPACE-complete — harder than NP-complete. The branching factor of valid pushes creates exponential state spaces. Modern Sokoban solvers use pattern databases and transposition tables. Human players rely on chunking and spatial reasoning that still outperforms brute-force search on many levels.
- `PUZ-010 pipedream` — Pipe placement under time pressure is online graph construction — building a path in a partially observable environment before knowing what pieces you'll receive. This is the stochastic planning problem: act now with incomplete future information. The game teaches players to build robust partial plans.
- `PUZ-016 code-crack` — Donald Knuth proved in 1977 that any code can be guessed in 5 moves or fewer using a minimax strategy. Each guess maximally partitions the remaining possibilities — this IS information entropy optimization, the same principle behind optimal decision trees and binary search.
- `PUZ-025 color-match` — Color Lines is a pure planning puzzle wrapped in a pathfinding constraint. The BFS reachability check transforms a simple matching game into a spatial logistics problem — every move must consider not just alignment but accessibility, and the random ball spawns create an ever-tightening noose of blocked paths.
- `PUZ-030 ball-maze` — Cameltry's genius is perceptual inversion. Instead of moving the player through the world, you rotate the world around the player. The marble obeys screen-space gravity while the maze rotates — a simple coordinate transform that creates deeply unintuitive yet learnable gameplay. The brain must decouple two reference frames simultaneously.
- `PUZ-033 grid-whack` — Quinty inverts the action-puzzle paradigm — instead of projectiles or direct attacks, the environment itself becomes the weapon. The floor tile as weapon mechanic is a precursor to the environmental interaction philosophy that would later define Game Freak's Pokemon (capturing and using the world around you). Enemy AI must path toward the player while avoiding tile-flip zones, creating emergent cat-and-mouse dynamics.
- `RAC-002 coast-racer` — Out Run's road rendering uses a segment-based projection system where each horizontal line of the road is a transformed strip — a scanline-based approach that creates convincing 3D from pure 2D math. This geometric transformation is structurally related to the projection operations in early computer vision systems.
- `RPG-001 party-rpg` — The dungeon crawl is a state-space search problem — the player explores a partially observable graph, building a mental model (map) through sequential observation. Party combat adds multi-agent coordination where each member's action must account for shared resources and complementary roles.
- `RPG-002 ys` — Ys eliminates the attack button entirely — combat IS movement. The player's approach angle determines damage, making spatial reasoning the only combat skill. This is a continuous action space reduced to a single verb (move), where positioning encodes all tactical decisions.
- `RPG-003 dungeon-crawl` — Gauntlet's monster generators create a producer-consumer dynamic — spawners produce threats faster than the player can eliminate them, forcing strategic prioritization of generators over individual enemies. This resource-pressure model appears in modern multi-agent swarm simulations.
- `RPG-004 open-rpg` — Ultima IV introduced a virtue system where the player's moral choices determined progression — the first game to use ethical behavior as a core mechanic. This value-alignment problem — ensuring the agent acts according to specified principles — is the central challenge of modern AI alignment research.
- `RPG-005 post-apoc-rpg` — Wasteland's skill system used probabilistic outcome resolution based on character stats — an early stochastic model where success depended on hidden dice rolls modified by invested skill points. This probability-weighted decision system mirrors the utility functions in decision-theoretic AI planning.
- `RPG-006 ascii-dungeon` — NetHack's procedural dungeon generation combined with its vast item interaction system creates emergent gameplay from deterministic rules — a combinatorial explosion of possibilities from simple components, presaging modern AI's interest in emergence from compositional systems.
- `RPG-007 party-crawler` — Dungeon Master pioneered real-time combat in first-person dungeon crawlers, replacing turn-based systems with time-pressure decision-making. The real-time element transforms tactical puzzle-solving into reactive resource management — a shift from deliberative to reactive AI paradigms.
- `SCR-001 defender` — Invented the world-larger-than-screen problem — a playable POMDP in 1981. The minimap is dimensionality reduction: compressing full world state into an interpretable summary, exactly what autoencoders (1986) and attention mechanisms (2017) do.
- `SCR-002 scramble` — First forced-scrolling shooter — the environment moves regardless of player input. This makes the game a real-time planning problem where future state is partially observable through the scrolling horizon. The dual weapon system (forward laser + downward bomb) creates a simple action space with positional context-dependent utility.
- `SCR-003 thexder` — Thexder's dual-mode design is a policy-switching problem — the player maintains two distinct control policies (walk vs. fly) and must select the appropriate one based on environmental context. The auto-aim laser in mech mode is an early example of assisted targeting, presaging modern aim-assist AI.
- `SCR-004 ikari` — Ikari Warriors separates movement from aiming — the player manages two independent control vectors simultaneously. This dual-output control problem mirrors multi-headed neural networks where separate output streams must coordinate toward a common objective.
- `SCR-005 warbird-scroll` — 1942's enemy formations follow scripted choreography — predetermined flight paths that create pattern-recognition challenges. This scripted-path approach to NPC behavior remains the foundation of shmup design and influenced waypoint-based AI navigation.
- `SCR-006 iso-shooter` — Zaxxon was the first arcade game to use axonometric projection, forcing players to reason about a 2.5D space using a shadow as altitude reference. This spatial-to-visual mapping problem — inferring 3D from 2D cues — is structurally identical to depth estimation challenges in computer vision.
- `SCR-008 space-dogfight` — Wing Commander's enemy fighters used behavior trees with personality parameters — some enemies would flee when damaged, others would kamikaze. Combined with wingman AI that responded to commands, it created one of gaming's first believable multi-agent combat simulations.
- `SCR-009 run-and-gun` — Contra's enemy spawning uses carefully choreographed patterns — soldiers run in from specific directions, turrets fire at timed intervals, and bosses have phased attack sequences. This scripted choreography creates the illusion of intelligent opposition through precise timing design.
- `SHT-001 swarm-rescue` — Twin-stick decoupled movement from attack for the first time. Before Robotron, you moved toward danger to fight it. After Robotron, you could flee and fight simultaneously. This single mechanical innovation birthed an entire genre and changed action game design forever. The family rescue mechanic added emotional stakes to pure survival.
- `SHT-004 corridor-shooter` — Doom's raycasting engine solved the 3D rendering problem by reducing it to 2D — casting rays from the player's viewpoint to determine wall distances. This dimensional reduction technique mirrors approaches in machine learning where high-dimensional problems are projected into lower dimensions for tractability.
- `SIM-002 utopia` — Utopia pioneered real-time resource management as gameplay — balancing food, gold, and population growth mirrors multi-variable optimization problems that expert systems were beginning to tackle in the early 1980s.
- `SIM-003 frontier-trek` — Oregon Trail pioneered educational simulation — using random event generation and resource depletion models to teach decision-making under uncertainty. The game's event system is essentially a simplified Monte Carlo simulation where each day samples from probability distributions.
- `SIM-005 god-sim` — Populous pioneered the god game genre where the player influences rather than directly controls — followers are autonomous agents that respond to environmental changes. This indirect control paradigm mirrors principal-agent problems in economics and multi-agent AI systems.
- `SIM-014 mule` — M.U.L.E. is a landmark in multiplayer economic game design. Its real-time auction mechanic — where buyers walk down and sellers walk up until they meet — elegantly encodes supply and demand into physical movement. Dani Bunten Berry pioneered cooperative-competitive design where player interaction IS the game, not combat overlaid on it. The AI opponents must balance resource production, cash reserves, and auction strategy — a multi-objective optimization problem that early expert systems approached with hand-tuned heuristics.
- `SIM-023 turnbased-rpg` — Hex grids create six-directional adjacency versus four for square grids, expanding tactical possibility space. The AI evaluates terrain bonuses and threat zones using heuristic rules — an expert system approach that mirrors how human players learn tactical principles through experience.
- `SPT-001 minigolf` — Mini golf is a trajectory planning problem with imperfect execution — the player must simulate ball physics mentally, accounting for reflections and friction. Each shot is a hypothesis about the physical world that is immediately tested. This predict-then-observe loop mirrors model-based reinforcement learning.
- `SPT-002 summer-games` — Each event is a different optimization problem sharing the same interface — the player must rapidly switch mental models between speed-maximization (mashing), timing-optimization (jumping), and angle-selection (throwing). This multi-task learning from a unified input space prefigures transfer learning across related domains.
- `SPT-003 skateboard` — The halfpipe creates a natural oscillator — the player's skill is in timing inputs to amplify the oscillation (pumping) and then exploiting peak amplitude (air time) to perform actions. This resonance-exploitation pattern appears in control theory as energy pumping in nonlinear oscillators.
- `SPT-004 slalom` — Slalom skiing is a sequential decision problem under momentum constraints — each gate approach must account for current velocity and the position of the NEXT gate. The player is solving a look-ahead path optimization where greedy choices (shortest path to current gate) may make the next gate unreachable.
- `SPT-007 boxing-ring` — Each opponent is a finite state machine with telegraph states. The player must perform real-time pattern recognition — identifying the tell, classifying the attack type, and executing the correct response. This is a supervised classification problem where the player is both the feature extractor and the classifier, learning from punishment rather than labeled data.
- `SPT-011 bowling` — Pin scatter is a chain-reaction physics problem: the ball hits the lead pin, which hits neighboring pins, which hit further pins. A perfect strike requires hitting the 1-3 pocket at the correct angle. The pin action is deterministic physics that feels chaotic — the same seeming randomness that emerges from simple physical rules.
- `SPT-013 carrom` — Carrom's physics puzzle is deceptively complex: multi-disc collisions create chaotic outcomes from precise inputs. An expert system can calculate ideal angles and power for single-shot pocketing, but planning multi-bounce combination shots requires search through continuous state spaces.
- `SPT-014 petanque` — Pétanque strategy is a classic example of the point-vs-shoot decision — a minimax problem where players choose between safe placement (pointing) and risky displacement (shooting). The optimal strategy depends on the current score differential and remaining boules, creating a dynamic programming problem similar to poker pot odds.
- `SPT-015 sepaktakraw` — Sepak takraw's acrobatic nature makes AI control fascinating — the bicycle kick requires precise timing of jump, rotation, and contact. Early sports game AI used simple heuristics for positioning; this game's AI mirrors that approach with rule-based court coverage and shot timing.
- `SPT-016 kabaddi` — Kabaddi's raid mechanic is a natural pursuit-evasion problem. Defenders must coordinate zone coverage while the raider optimizes a path that maximizes tags under a strict time constraint — a variant of the travelling salesman problem with adversarial obstacles and a hard deadline.
- `SPT-018 ddakji` — Ddakji's flip mechanic is a fascinating physics puzzle where air displacement and impact force combine. The optimal slam angle creates maximum air pressure under the target card's edge. An expert system can model the probability curve but the real-world chaos of paper aerodynamics makes perfect play impossible — a satisfying blend of skill and chance.
- `SPT-020 ssireum` — Ssireum reduces to a balance prediction problem. Each wrestler is a dynamic system with a center of gravity — techniques shift this balance, and the opponent must counter-predict. The AI opponent uses a simple FSM with weighted random technique selection biased by its own balance state, mirroring early game AI heuristics.
- `SRV-001 bar-serve` — Tapper is a real-time multi-queue scheduling problem identical to CPU task dispatch, elevator routing, and later RL environments. The player IS the scheduler — choosing between N queues with different urgency levels and no lookahead. John Miller's bartender intuition ran on the same algorithm as Dijkstra's scheduler.
- `SRV-003 food-stack` — BurgerTime's enemies use simple pursuit algorithms but the multi-level platform structure creates emergent complexity — the player must plan paths that manipulate enemy positions while completing the burger assembly objective.
- `SRV-004 delivery-run` — Paperboy is a multi-objective optimization problem — the player simultaneously maximizes deliveries (accuracy), minimizes damage (avoidance), and manages limited resources (papers). The subscription mechanic adds a meta-game where current performance affects future difficulty, creating a feedback loop between skill and challenge.
- `TRP-001 area-claim` — Territorial control where your own line is the threat zone — a multi-armed bandit with spatial, temporal, and risk dimensions. Claiming territory (exploitation) requires exposing your incomplete line (exploration cost). Adversarial minimax with continuous state space.
- `TRP-003 split-bounce` — JezzBall is a real-time spatial partitioning problem. Each wall divides the space into regions that must be evaluated for ball containment. The optimal strategy requires reasoning about area maximization under dynamic constraints — the same computational geometry problems that spatial indexing (BSP trees, quadtrees) solves. The player performs real-time geometric reasoning that maps directly to algorithms developed for computer graphics.
- `TRP-006 libblerabble` — Two-point cooperative enclosure requires coordinating two agents to form topological containment. The string-wrapping mechanic is computational geometry (convex hull, winding number) embedded in real-time play. Namco founder Masaya Nakamura supervised — his vision of games as spatial puzzles with social dimensions.
- `FIX-009 sky-intercept` — Missile Command is fundamentally a resource allocation problem under time pressure. The intercept optimization overlay reveals the hidden math: which incoming missiles threaten which cities, where the optimal intercept point is for each trajectory, and how to prioritize limited counter-missiles. This is operations research applied to arcade defense — the same class of problem solved by linear programming and assignment algorithms since the 1940s.
- `MAZ-018 light-territory` — Territory control in Tron is a spatial Voronoi problem — every empty cell belongs to whichever player can reach it first. The overlay reveals that winning Tron is less about chasing your opponent and more about maximizing your reachable space. This is the same insight that modern competitive Tron AIs use.
- `MAZ-020 tunnel-ai` — Dig Dug's tunnel system is a player-authored maze. Every dig decision reshapes the game's topology — creating new paths for enemies and new trap opportunities. The overlay makes the strategic value of each possible dig direction visible, turning intuitive excavation into legible spatial reasoning.
- `MAZ-021 tile-route` — The BFS overlay reveals that human players rarely take the optimal route. We satisfice — picking the nearest visible item rather than computing global shortest paths. The numbered priority overlay externalizes what a perfect solver would do, letting you compare your intuition against brute-force optimality.
- `MAZ-022 stalker-prox` — Night Stalker's terror comes from not knowing how close enemies are around blind corners. The proximity heatmap externalizes the spatial awareness that skilled players develop intuitively — transforming gut-feel danger sense into a computed distance field. Weapon scarcity adds resource planning: the overlay shows not just where danger is, but where safety (weapons) will appear.
- `MAZ-023 burrow-path` — Mole Mania's two-layer system creates a dual-graph problem. The surface has obstacles and the ball, the underground has shortcuts. The optimal solution requires reasoning about which dig points connect useful underground passages to strategic surface positions for ball-pushing. The overlay makes this cross-layer routing visible.
- `PLT-024 barrel-dodge` — Expert Donkey Kong players unconsciously predict barrel paths 2-3 seconds ahead, choosing ladder timing based on gap windows. This overlay makes that intuitive pattern recognition visible — showing how the brain builds physics models of rolling objects on slopes and uses them for planning.
- `PLT-025 whip-fsm` — Castlevania enemies use finite state machines — discrete behavioral states with explicit transition rules. The FSM overlay makes this architecture visible: each enemy displays its current state, the conditions that would trigger transitions, and the predicted next action. This reveals that classic game AI is rule-based expert system design, not learning.
- `PLT-026 jungle-danger` — Pitfall's hazards operate on deterministic cycles — pits open and close, logs roll at fixed intervals, vines swing predictably. The temporal danger zone overlay makes these cycles visible as timeline bars, turning implicit pattern memorization into explicit temporal reasoning. Players see WHEN each hazard will be safe, transforming reflex play into strategic timing.
- `PUZ-041 codebreak-knuth` — Mastermind IS information theory. Each guess partitions the solution space; Knuth's minimax algorithm chooses the guess that minimizes the worst-case partition size. The overlay reveals that code-breaking is really about asking the most informative question.
- `PUZ-043 boxpush-deadlock` — Sokoban is PSPACE-complete — the state space grows exponentially with grid size. Deadlock detection prunes impossible branches before they waste the player's time. A dead square is any position from which no sequence of pushes can ever reach a target. The overlay reveals the invisible walls of the solution space.
- `PUZ-044 ballmaze-path` — Cameltry inverts the maze paradigm — instead of moving through the maze, you rotate the maze around the marble. The path prediction overlay reveals that this is really a continuous gravity-vector problem: at every angle, the marble's future path is deterministic given current velocity and maze geometry.
- `PUZ-048 colormatch-path` — Color Lines is secretly a logistics puzzle. The path exists or it doesn't — BFS determines reachability in O(n) time. But the strategic depth lies in alignment scoring: each legal move should be evaluated not just by whether a path exists, but by how it advances toward 5-in-a-row. The overlay reveals this dual structure — pathfinding for feasibility, pattern matching for strategy.
- `SCR-016 run-predict` — Contra's difficulty comes from memorized spawn patterns. Expert players know exactly when and where enemies will appear because the game follows deterministic schedules tied to scroll position. The overlay makes this hidden schedule visible, showing spawn warnings, bullet trajectories, and safe corridors — revealing the pattern knowledge that separates veterans from newcomers.
- `SCR-017 warbird-formations` — 1942's enemy formations follow scripted choreography — predetermined V-shapes, lines, circles, and crosses. The overlay deconstructs these formations, revealing the geometric structure, threat density, and safe corridors that skilled players learn to read intuitively. Pattern analysis transforms implicit knowledge into visible structure.
- `SPT-029 bowling-scatter` — Ancient bowling had no physics engines. Modern bowling analysis uses high-speed cameras and physics simulations to predict pin action — which pins hit which, at what angles, with what energy transfer. This overlay makes that invisible physics visible: before you throw, you can see the predicted chain reaction, turning intuition into information.

**Era 3-4** — 3-4 (Late Knowledge / Early Neural Revival — circa 1985)
- `PUZ-001 falling-blocks` — Real-time NP-hard combinatorial optimization. Appears to have no game ancestry — like the hardest AI problems, it emerged from mathematics, not from iterating on prior games. Tetris AI remains an active research area.

**Era 3-5** — 3-5 (Knowledge Explosion → Statistical — Bayesian search, probability distributions, hunt/target modes)
- `HYB-005 naval-grid` — Optimal Battleship play is Bayesian inference. Given known hits and misses, the probability distribution of remaining ship positions can be computed. The transition from "hunt mode" (random probing) to "target mode" (focused searching around a hit) mirrors the explore-exploit tradeoff in reinforcement learning.
- `PLT-015 cinematic-escape` — Éric Chahi's solo creation of Another World proved that a single developer with the right tools (rotoscoping, vector animation, a custom VM) could rival teams of dozens. This prefigures how AI-augmented solo creators can now build entire game worlds — the constraint of one mind forcing elegant, coherent design.
- `HYB-014 naval-bayes` — Battleship is a search problem. Every shot is a Bayesian update: misses eliminate possibilities, hits constrain ship orientations. The probability heatmap makes this explicit — showing the posterior distribution over ship placements given all observed evidence. Watch how a single hit transforms the uniform prior into sharp spikes on adjacent cells. This is hunt-target mode visualized as mathematics.
- `MAZ-011 gapfinder` — Frogger is a rhythm game disguised as a maze game. Each lane has a periodic pattern with gaps. The gap-finder overlay shows what expert players see: the timing window for each lane — when it's safe to cross, when it's about to become safe, and when it's dangerous. This is temporal pattern recognition — predicting future states from observed periodicity.
- `RAC-005 routemind` — Out Run's branching paths are a decision tree. Each fork offers two futures with different expected outcomes. The route mind overlay computes what a planning algorithm would: the expected value of each branch based on traffic, distance, time pressure, and difficulty. Yu Suzuki designed the branches so that harder paths reward more — a natural reward-shaping incentive structure.
- `SCR-011 foglifter` — Defender invented the minimap as dimensionality reduction — compressing a wide world into a small radar display. The fog-lifter goes further: it shows enemy threat vectors, predicted intercept points, and a belief state about what's happening off-screen. This is the POMDP solved visually: maintaining a probability distribution over hidden states that the agent (player) cannot directly observe.
- `SHT-010 corridor-spatial` — Doom's magic was making 2D feel 3D. But underneath the illusion, id Software used BSP trees — a spatial partitioning algorithm from computational geometry. This overlay strips away the texture-mapped fantasy and shows the spatial reasoning underneath: which enemies are most dangerous, what path leads to the exit, and where death zones cluster. It transforms a reflex game into a strategic puzzle.

**Era 3-6** — 3-6 (Knowledge Explosion → Deep Learning — sequence modeling, LSTM, attention mechanisms)
- `TRP-004 simon` — Simon tests sequential working memory — the same capacity that LSTM networks and transformer attention model. Each round adds one element, linearly growing the sequence. Human memory typically fails around 7±2 items (Miller's Law, 1956). The game is a direct measurement of working memory span.
- `MAZ-010 ghostmind` — Each ghost's personality is a hand-crafted reward function: Blinky targets your position (greedy pursuit), Pinky targets 4 tiles ahead (ambush planning), Inky uses Blinky's position for flanking (cooperative MARL), Clyde scatters when close (risk-averse policy). The overlay makes these invisible policies visible — showing what players intuit but can't articulate.
- `PLT-013 bounty-map` — Metroid's world is a directed graph where abilities are keys and obstacles are locks. The coverage overlay shows the reachability analysis: which rooms are accessible now, which require abilities you don't have yet, and the shortest path to unlock new areas. This is the same state-space search that AI planners use — computing which actions (abilities) unlock which states (rooms).
- `SHT-007 threatmap` — In a swarm of 50+ enemies, which one will kill you? The threat overlay computes what expert players intuit: priority ranking based on distance, speed, trajectory convergence, and group density. This is potential field navigation — the same algorithm robots use to avoid obstacles. The danger heatmap shows the survival topology of the arena.

**Era 3-7** — 3-7 (Rule Systems through Modern AI — from early autonomous agents to sophisticated virtual life simulation)
- `SIM-015 virtualpet` — Little Computer People was one of the first games where AI characters exhibited autonomous behavior — making their own decisions based on internal needs. This approach presaged modern virtual pet AI and The Sims' need-driven autonomy, where agents prioritize actions based on weighted utility functions.
- `RPG-009 virtuescope` — Ultima IV replaced "maximize score" with "demonstrate virtue" — the player is judged by ethical behavior, not combat kills. This IS reward shaping through human feedback, formalized as RLHF 35 years later. The virtue scope makes the hidden reward function visible — showing exactly how each choice modifies your alignment, just as RLHF shows how human preferences shape model behavior.

**Era 4** — 4 (Expert Boom — finite state machines, sensor models)
- `ADV-007 stealth` — Guard AI in stealth games implements a perception-decision-action loop: sensor model (vision cone), state machine (patrol/alert/search/chase), and behavior rules. This is the same observe-orient-decide-act (OODA) loop that military AI research formalized. The player learns to exploit each stage — staying outside perception, manipulating state transitions, predicting patrol routes.
- `ANC-009 reversi` — Corner squares are maximally valuable (can never be flipped), edges are strong, squares adjacent to corners are dangerous. This positional hierarchy maps directly to feature-weighted evaluation functions used in all game AI.
- `DEF-006 gem-aim` — Puchi Carat merged Breakout with competitive puzzle-game attack mechanics. The AI opponent must balance offense (clearing gem clusters for attacks) with defense (surviving incoming garbage). This dual-objective optimization presages modern game AI that must weigh multiple competing goals simultaneously.
- `DEF-007 tontie` — Tontie maps a 3×3 spatial grid to the numpad, creating a learned motor mapping between visual space and finger positions. This is a form of stimulus-response compatibility — the numpad's physical layout mirrors the game grid, making the mapping intuitive once learned. The game becomes a test of how fast your brain can translate visual position to motor action.
- `FIX-008 fortress` — Fortress elevated artillery games with wind as a core variable — every shot requires recalculating the parabolic arc under changing conditions. The AI must solve a noisy optimization problem each turn, balancing weapon choice, wind compensation, and terrain destruction strategy.
- `HYB-011 bishibashi` — Bishi Bashi's rapid minigame format is a fascinating AI challenge — an agent must instantly recognize a new game context, identify the correct strategy, and execute with precision, all within seconds. This mirrors transfer learning and few-shot adaptation in modern AI. The compilation format also demonstrates how simple mechanics become complex through rapid context-switching.
- `PHY-006 artillery-duel` — Worms' destructible terrain turned the environment from static backdrop into dynamic game state. Every shot permanently altered the possibility space — a concept that parallels how reinforcement learning agents must adapt to non-stationary environments.
- `PUZ-002 columns` — Columns is a constraint satisfaction problem over a 2D grid with falling pieces. Unlike Tetris (spatial packing), Columns is about color alignment — a different NP-hard problem. Match-3 became the foundation of a $10B mobile game category, proving that color matching is a universal cognitive primitive.
- `PUZ-003 incredible-machine` — The Incredible Machine is a constraint satisfaction problem in continuous physics space — the player must reason about how objects will interact under gravity and collision, placing components to create a valid chain from start to goal. This is planning in a physics simulator, a challenge that modern AI still struggles with.
- `PUZ-005 march-rescue` — Lemmings are autonomous agents with minimal AI — they walk, turn at walls, and fall off edges. The player's role is essentially programming behavior modifications (dig, build, block) onto individual agents, making this one of the first mainstream agent-programming puzzles.
- `PUZ-008 minesweeper` — Minesweeper is NP-complete. Each numbered cell is a constraint; solving the board is constraint satisfaction. Players perform Boolean inference intuitively — the same operation that formal CSP solvers automate. The game teaches logical deduction through play.
- `PUZ-012 nonogram` — Nonogram solving is NP-complete in general, but most published puzzles are designed to be solvable by constraint propagation alone — line solving without backtracking. The puzzle teaches players systematic constraint reasoning: each row/column is an independent constraint that must agree with all orthogonal constraints.
- `PUZ-032 feed-match` — Baku Baku adds a predator-prey matching layer atop the falling-block formula. Instead of color matching, the AI must reason about typed relationships — animals eat specific foods. This asymmetric pairing creates a richer search space than simple color alignment, requiring the AI to track which block types enable future cascades.
- `PUZ-034 drop-match` — Magical Drop's grab-stack-throw mechanic creates an optimization problem unique among puzzle games. Unlike Tetris or Puyo Puyo where pieces arrive and must be placed, here the player actively harvests and redistributes existing pieces — a real-time constraint satisfaction problem where speed of recognition and execution dominates strategy.
- `PUZ-035 swap-match` — Panel de Pon's chain system creates an emergent complexity curve — setting up multi-chain combos requires spatial prediction and timing that rewards both human intuition and AI lookahead. The rising floor adds constant pressure that transforms a matching puzzle into a real-time strategy game.
- `PUZ-038 moneyexchanger` — Money Idol Exchanger turns arithmetic into spatial reasoning. The exchange rules mirror real currency — five 1s make a 5, two 5s make a 10 — so the mechanic is instantly intuitive to anyone who has handled money. The cascade potential (1¥ all the way to 500¥) creates a combinatorial explosion that rewards long-term planning over reactive play.
- `RAC-004 kart` — Mario Kart pioneered rubber-banding — AI that adapts difficulty based on player performance. This dynamic difficulty adjustment predated formal research into adaptive systems and remains one of gaming's most effective engagement mechanisms.
- `RPG-008 dungeon-loot` — Diablo's procedural dungeon generation combined with randomized loot created a variable-ratio reinforcement schedule — the same psychological mechanism behind slot machines. This marriage of procedural content and reward psychology defined the action RPG genre.
- `SIM-004 empire-builder` — Civilization's AI must balance exploration, expansion, economic development, military production, and diplomacy simultaneously — a multi-objective optimization problem that mirrors the challenges of real-world resource allocation that operations research had been studying for decades.
- `SIM-008 tactics` — Turn-based tactics games are discrete optimization problems where terrain transforms the payoff matrix. The AI must evaluate not just unit matchups but positional advantage — a spatial reasoning challenge that connects to classical game tree search with evaluation functions informed by domain knowledge.
- `SIM-018 denshadego` — Train driving simulators are constrained optimization problems: arrive on time, stop precisely, obey speed limits. The tension between schedule pressure and braking physics creates a satisfying control loop where mastery means internalizing the dynamics model — the same challenge faced by real-time control systems.
- `SIM-022 kingdomwinds` — The Kingdom of the Winds pioneered the Korean MMO model that would dominate Asian gaming for decades. Jake Song's design married single-player RPG depth with persistent multiplayer worlds. The quest-kill-loot-return loop became the foundational grammar of online RPGs worldwide. This prototype distills that loop into its purest form.
- `SPT-005 slope-run` — SkiFree's infamous Abominable Snowman is a chase agent with a simple but effective catch-up algorithm — it always runs faster than the player, making escape feel impossible but creating a memorable emergent narrative. The yeti became a cultural touchstone for unexpected game AI behavior.
- `ANC-024 reversi-weights` — Reversi/Othello AI was revolutionized by positional weight matrices. The Logistello program (1997) defeated world champion Takeshi Murakami 6-0 using carefully tuned evaluation functions. Corners are worth +120 because they can never be flipped; X-squares (diagonal to corners) are -40 because they give the opponent corner access. This overlay makes the AI's strategic knowledge visible.
- `FGT-008 arena-combos` — Combo systems in fighting games are essentially formal grammars — a set of rules defining which move sequences are valid. The combo overlay makes this grammar visible, showing the tree of possible continuations after each hit. Frame advantage determines which branches are reachable, mirroring how parsers determine valid next tokens in a language.
- `PUZ-047 swap-chain` — Panel de Pon's chain system rewards setting up cascading matches before they happen. The chain overlay simulates every possible swap, runs the full gravity-and-match cascade, and reports the chain depth — turning intuitive stacking into visible combinatorial planning.
- `PUZ-049 feedmatch-feed` — Baku Baku's feeding mechanic hides a cascade prediction problem — each animal-food adjacency triggers consumption that causes blocks above to fall, potentially creating new adjacencies. The overlay simulates every possible drop column, runs the full cascade chain, and displays predicted cascade depth, revealing how a simple matching game contains recursive chain reaction analysis.
- `PUZ-050 dropmatch-opt` — Magical Drop's frantic speed hides a rich optimization problem. Each grab-throw decision involves evaluating which column offers the most same-color drops to grab, and which column would create the largest match when those drops are thrown. The overlay reveals the greedy analysis: highlighting the best grab column, the best throw destination, and the predicted clear count — turning a reflex game into a visible decision tree.
- `PUZ-051 money-cascade` — Money Exchanger hides a denomination tree beneath its falling-coin surface. Each placement can trigger a chain reaction — five 1¥ coins become one 5¥, which might complete a pair of 5¥ to make 10¥, and so on up to 500¥. The cascade overlay reveals this tree, showing how deep each column placement will chain before you drop. This transforms reactive play into strategic planning — you're not just matching coins, you're engineering avalanches through a currency graph.
- `WLD-003 neural-game` — The learning adversary demonstrates what ML researchers in the 1980s discovered: a system that updates its beliefs from observed patterns forces its opponent to discover strategies the system hasn't modeled yet. The game is an arms race. The arms race IS the game.

**Era 4-5** — 4-5 (Neural Revival → Statistical Learning — TD-Gammon by Gerald Tesauro, temporal difference learning)
- `ANC-010 backgammon` — TD-Gammon (1992) achieved expert-level play through self-play and temporal difference learning — predicting the value of a position by comparing it to the next position. This was a precursor to AlphaZero's approach. The dice add stochastic elements that require expectimax rather than minimax.
- `ANC-027 backgammon-ev` — TD-Gammon (1992) learned to play expert-level Backgammon entirely through self-play using temporal difference learning. It discovered strategies that surprised human experts, proving neural networks could master games with stochastic elements. The EV overlay makes visible the position evaluation TD-Gammon learned — showing why certain moves are strong despite appearing counterintuitive.

**Era 4-6** — 4-6 (Expert Boom → Deep Learning — pattern recognition, sequence prediction, frame analysis)
- `FGT-007 framereader` — Fighting game AI traditionally "input reads" — it reacts to your button presses faster than humanly possible. The frame reader overlay makes this visible: it shows the AI's prediction of your next move, the frame advantage of each attack, and which attacks are safe or unsafe on block. This is the same sequence prediction that RNNs and transformers perform — predicting the next token (move) from the sequence (match history).
- `PUZ-014 probefield` — Each numbered cell in Minesweeper is a constraint. Solving is Boolean inference — the same CSP algorithms used in AI planning.
